
# The Old Canoe

### 208 reprints from 1857-12-31 to 1899-08-25

## [The Old Canoe](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn82014141/1860-03-01/ed-1/seq-1/)

#### From _St. Charles City Republican Intelligencer_

#### 1860-03-01

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

**THE OLD CANOE.**  
  
**BY EMILY R. PAGE.**  
  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,    
And the waters below look dark and deep;    
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,    
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;    
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,    
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;    
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;,    
Lies at its mooring, the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,    
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lopped,    
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,    
Like folded hands when the work is done;    
While busily back and forth between,    
The spider stretches his silvery screen,    
And the solemn owl with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;    
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,    
Rots slowly away, in its living grave;    
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,    
Hiding the mouldering dust away,    
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,    
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;    
While many a blossom of loveliest hue    
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The current waters are dead and still,    
But the light winds play with the boat at will;    
And lazily in and out again,    
It floats the length of the rusty chain,    
Like the weary march of the hands of time,    
That meet and part at the noontide chime;    
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew    
By the dipping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,    
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,    
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,    
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,    
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,    
And looked below in the broken tide,    
To see that the faces and boats were two,    
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,    
And look below in the sluggish tide,    
The face that I see there is graver grown,    
And the laugh that I hear has a sobered tone,    
And the hand that lent to the light skiff wings    
Have grown familiar with sterner things;    
But I love to think of the hours that flew,    
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,    
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss grew,    
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
  
Bradford, Vt.  

</td></tr></table>

---

#### 1858-03-26 &middot; [Biddeford [Me.]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Biddeford%2C_Maine)

Of the icebergs round them meet,  
Pressing closer till they sunk crashing to the  
deep?-  
Do these crystal mountains loom,  
Monuments for that vast tomb  
In the Ocean&#x27;s quiet depths where so many sleep?  
O thou ever surging sea,  
Vainly do we question thee:  
The blue waves no answer bring as they kiss the  
strand,  
But we know each coral grave,  
Far beneath the rolling wave,  
Shall at last give up its dead touched by God&#x27;s  
right hand.

THE OLD CANOE.  
nY EMILY R. PAGE.  
In verse we have never seen anything more to  
the life than this. The moral, too, is a touching  
one. -Ed.  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the ragged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide:  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank­  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath  
lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is doue;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with a dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave;  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still­  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runt  
quick-  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe!  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide.  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe

Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank—  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lays at its moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm hath lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
  
While busily back and forth between,  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘+ too hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
O, many a time, with a careless hand,  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick—  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe!  
  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling sido,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss grew  
  
O’er the moulderiug stern of the old canoe.

## [From _The liberator._](https://archive.org/details/sim_liberator_1859-10-21_29_42/page/n3/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1859-10-21 &middot; [Boston, Mass.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Boston)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
THE OLD CANOE.  
  
BY MISS EMILY 3B. PAGE.  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through—  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddies are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea-bird’s wing that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn ow), with his dull ‘ too-hoo,’  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that piants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower ;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime ;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the Gripping bow of the old cance.  
  
O, many a time, with a careless hand,  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—  
Where the whirls are wild, and the eddies are thick—  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/iiif/sim_liberator_1859-10-21_29_42&#0036;3/pct:7.751938,8.231132,14.098837,29.186321/,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

## From _Nashville union and American._

#### 1859-11-16 &middot; [Nashville, Tenn.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Nashville%2C_Tennessee)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

The following lines, which we clip from the 4r-  
kansas State Garette, are full of genuine poetry:  
The OlD Canee.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Loans gloomily over the murky tide:  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lays at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles areidly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lepped,  
And crosed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Lke folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay.  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Bprings up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and stili  
Bat the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It fleats the length of its rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canos.  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick  
Where the whiris are wild and the eddies are thick  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that tho faces and boats were two,  
hat were mirrered back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the slugglish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings, I  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that fiew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw,  
Are the blonsoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the siouldering stern of the old canoe.
</td></tr></table>

THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
bays at its mooring the old eanoc.  
The uscless paddles are idly droppod,  
Like a sca bivd&#x27;s wings that the storm hath  
lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
jettles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hid ng the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still-  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out agkin,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That mect and part at the moontide chime;  
And the shore is k&#x27;ssed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the od canoe.  
, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pébbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick-  
Where the whirls aro wild and the eddies are  
thick-  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To seesthat the faces of the boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoc.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side;  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _The Nevada Democrat._](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn84026881/1860-01-25/ed-1/seq-4)

#### 1860-01-25 &middot; [Nevada, Calif.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Nevada_City%2C_California)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks arc gray and tka abort la steep,  
And the water* below look dark and deep,  
Wbei e the rugged pine, in it* lonely pride,  
Lean* gloomily oeer the murky tide ;  
Where the reed* and runhe* are long and rank,  
And the wee da grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the thadow ia heary the whole day through,  
Lite at it* mooring* the old canoe.  
The ntelesa paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a tea-bird’* wing* that the storm hat lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, on* o’er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work la done;  
While buiaily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches hit silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old saaoe.  
The stern hall stink in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in Its living grave,  
And the green moss creep* o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that piaote * &#x27;re the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivey that mantle* the tailing tower;  
While many a blosom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
it floats the length of its rusty chain,  
like the weary march of the hand of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebly strand ;  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick—  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And louked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the face* and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old cone*.  
Rut now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there I* graver grown,  
And the laugh that I bear has a sober tons,  
And the hands that lent to the light skilf wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things:  
But I love to think of the hours that Hew  
As 2 rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ire the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
U’er the mouldering stern ot the old canoe

## From _St. Charles City Republican intelligencer._

#### 1860-03-01 &middot; [St. Charles City [Charles City], Iowa](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Charles_City%2C_Iowa)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;,  
Lies at its moorings, the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lopped,  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Setties down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly a way, in its living grave  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb.  
  
a flower,  
  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will;  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dipping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
  
And laughod as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sobered tone,  
And the hand that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
Bradford, Vt.
</td></tr></table>

The following beautiful lines have been going  
the rounds of newspaperdom for several years  
without credit. Whoever the author is. and  
we confess our inability to name him, he should  
claim the wandering progeny of bis brain, as  
they are worthy of the palmiest days of genuine  
poetry:  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep

## From _Holmes County farmer._

#### 1860-07-12 &middot; [Millersburg, Ohio](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Millersburg%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
e e e  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomingly over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and the rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wing that the wind hath lop&#x27;d,  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done:  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its full decay,  
Hiding its smouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue,  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe,  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pulled it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and forms were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide.  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that flew, Ithrew,  
As I rock&#x27;d where the whirls their white spray  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  

</td></tr></table>

## The Old Canoe

#### From _Newark advocate._

#### 1861-08-16 &middot; [Newark [Ohio]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Newark%2C_Ohio)

[This text is not available under an open license.]

---

## From _Holmes County farmer._

#### 1861-09-26 &middot; [Millersburg, Ohio](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Millersburg%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through  
Lays at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lopped.  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen;  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue,  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at w  
And lazi y in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pulled it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick  
And I laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And looked below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the skiff light wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
threw.  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  

</td></tr></table>

---

The Old Canoe.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep ;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride.  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
Where the shadaw is heavy the whole day through  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lopped,  
  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done ;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches hia silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rota slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless wafers are dead and still  
But the light winds play with the boat at will.  
And lazily in and out again.  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of Time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew.  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
0, many a time with a careless hand  
1 have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick.  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er its rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and bouts were two. .  
Bnt now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone.  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings.  
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where tlie whirls their wild spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe

## From _Holmes County farmer._

#### 1863-04-23 &middot; [Millersburg, Ohio](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Millersburg%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide  
When the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thich on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;,  
Lays at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lopped.  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the toomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
I  
Springs up o&#x27;r the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in end out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pulled it away from the pebly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick  
Where the whirls are wild and the addies are  
thick  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
And the hand that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to talk of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirl their wild spray  
threw,  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern of the old canoe.&quot;
</td></tr></table>

Wbete the rock* are pray and the slope U sleep.  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the ragged pine in its lonely pride.  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reek and rushes are tall and rank.  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow la heavy the whole day through.  
Lay at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped.  
Like a seabird&#x27;s wings that the storm had lopped,  
And crossed on the railings, one o’er one.  
Like folded hands when the work ia done:  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches her silver screen.  
And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘too-hoo,”  
Settles down oa the side of the old canoe.  
0. many a time, when a careless hand,  
I hare pushed it away from the pebbly strand.  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirles are wild and the eddies are thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boat* were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I luve to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw.  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old cauoe.

Poetry.  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the slope is sleep.  
And the waters below look dark and deep ;  
Where the ragged pine, in its lonely pride,  
T.cans gloomy over the murky tide :  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall tnd rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the w inding bank ;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lay at its mooring the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like the seabird’s wing that the storm had lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily hack and forth between,  
The spider stretches her silver screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
0, many a time, with a careless ’ and,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand)  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two.  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now as I loan o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And lock below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver gro.rn,  
And the laugh that I hear lias a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown f,miliar with sterner things ;  
But 1 love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw,  
Kre the blossoms waved or the green grass grew,  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe

THE OLD CANOE.  
We take great pleasure in reproducing this ex­  
quisite poem, cut many years ago from an obscure  
Arkansas paper, and published in the North.—  
Every now and then it re-appears on the surface of  
newspaper literature. It desires a more prominent  
place in our letters :  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
&#x27; And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The uselesss paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done ;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tonib a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;  
While many a blossom of the loveliest hue,  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waves are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the wenry march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime.  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew.  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand ;  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
And now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skill&#x27; wings,  
Have grown familial- with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stem of the old canoe

## From _Walla Walla statesman._

#### 1865-08-11 &middot; [Walla Walla, Wash. Terr.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Walla_Walla%2C_Washington)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

 7  
If he&#x27;s not common sense,  
And can boast a few pence,  
Lift him up.  
If his face shows no shame,  
Lift him up, lift him up;  
Though crime is his name,  
Lift him up;  
Though disgrace be his sport  
Let your daughters him court-  
Lift him up.  
aftass eset hoimrsotonI  
 woman.ie  
If woman once errs,  
Kick her down, kick her down;  
If misfortune is hers,  
Kick her down;  
Though her tears fall like rain,  
And she ne&#x27;er smiles again,  
Kick her down.  
If man breaks her heart,  
Kick her down, kick her down,  
Redouble the smart,  
Kick her down;  
And if low in condition,  
On, on to perdition,  
Kick her down!  
oO  
The Old Canoe.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day throu&#x27;,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-birds wings that the storm has lopped  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe,  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Roots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles a falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are deep and still,  
But the light wind plays on the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain;  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anewh  
By the dripping how of the old canoe.  
O, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand;  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick, bl anst agad baulasgtngede  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe,  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side, at  
And look below on the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
As I rocked where the whirls their light spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _Staunton spectator._](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn84024718/1867-06-25/ed-1/seq-1)

#### 1867-06-25 &middot; [Staunton, Va.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Staunton%2C_Virginia)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

f either of the other papers.  
  
Foetry.  
For the Spectator.  
The River.  
Be still, ever flowing, never quiet, river,  
Cease to upbraid me as you hurry along;  
Taunt me no longer with joys gone forever,  
Hush, O ! hush thy sorrowful and mournful song.  
But ever receding and never heeding,  
The cruel river with my hopes is fleeting.  
Away! Away! with memories of the past,  
Divide me no longer with fears that have fled,  
Bury all the happiness that could not last,  
Hide, O! hide the hopes and desires that are  
dead.  
But ever and on the river is flowing,  
Heedless of all my agony, is going.  
_#-. .  
The Old Canoe.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep :  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Nestles down on the side of the old can«e.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern ofthe old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick,  
Where the whirls were wild and the storm was  
thick;  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the brokeji tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide  
The face that I see is graver grown,  
And the laugh I heard has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
threw;  
Ere the blossoms moved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern ofthe old canoe.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/iiif/2/vi_lure_ver01%2Fdata%2Fsn84024718%2F00280762477%2F1867062501%2F0315.jp2/pct:31.957490,18.716210,12.647176,36.455978/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

#### 1867-07-23 &middot; [New York [N.Y.]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

PoetrY  
THE OLD CANOR,  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide:  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-birds wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-b  
Nestles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Itots slowly in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
If ling the mouldering dust away.  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that maniles a fallen tower;  
While many a blssom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old cance.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain.  
Lke the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And piddled it down where the streamn ran quick,  
Where the whirls were wild and the storm was  
thick;  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I heard has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff win  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms moyed or the green grass gre  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.

Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep.  
And the waters below look dark and deep.

And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe. Ithrough,  
The useless paddies are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one, &quot;  
Like folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Nestles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slot,3n its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the moldering dust away.  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hand of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick,  
Where the whirls were wild and the storm was  
  
thick!  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I heard has a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
Bat I love to think of the hours that flew, fthrew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray  
Ere the blossoms moved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

## The Old Canoe

#### From _Daily evening bulletin._

#### 1867-12-31 &middot; [San Francisco [Calif.]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/San_Francisco)

## From _The Plymouth weekly Democrat._

#### 1868-02-27 &middot; [Plymouth, Ind.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Plymouth%2C_Indiana)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are grey and the shore is stecp,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lowly pride  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank:  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at its moorings the Old Canoe,  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm had lopped,  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the Old Canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue,  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the Old Canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noon-tide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dripping bow of the Old Canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand.  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quiek,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And I laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the Old Canoe.  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side  
And look below at the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has alsoberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings.  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
As I rocked where the winds their wild spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the Old Canoe
</td></tr></table>

SATURDAY MORNING, APRIL 2, 1870.  
  
Select Poetrp.  
THIE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through  
Lies at its moorings the old canoc.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery sereen  
And the solemn owl with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimmy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay  
Hiding its moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower  
While many a blossom of lovliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoc.  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the streams runs  
quiok,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe
</td></tr></table>

iunelO- of Albert J. Wheat, dec&#x27;d.

STAUNTON, VA., TUESDAY, AUGUST 26, 1873.  
  
  
.Yoetry.  
The Old Canoe.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep.  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomly over the murky tide ;  
Where the reads and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,  
There lies at its moorings the old.canoe.  
The useless piddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopp­  
ed,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore i 3 kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where tho whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces end boats were two,  
That were mirrored b.ick from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that 1 hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wiugs  
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;  
But 1 love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/iiif/2/vi_lure_ver01%2Fdata%2Fsn84024718%2F00280762490%2F1873082601%2F0530.jp2/pct:29.657019,11.510111,35.177866,86.014351/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>

#### 1873-09-03 &middot; [Somerset, Pa.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Somerset%2C_Pennsylvania)

SOMERSET, PA. WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 3, 1875.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
 eeeeeee eae  
The following fine verses appeared  
anonymously some years ago in a pa-  
per at Little Rock, Arkansas. The  
writter could have well afforded to  
give his or her name with it.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide:  
Where the reeds and the rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done:  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silver screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of lovilest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoc.  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below at the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wing  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew,  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
MY BACHELOR UNCLE
</td></tr></table>

## From _The home journal._

#### 1873-09-04 &middot; [Winchester, Tenn.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Winchester%2C_Tennessee)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

lf the social questions that vex our  
souls will be answered when the world  
concedes that a woman is a normal, re  
sponsible individual; that she must be  
the protector of her own honor, the  
judge of her own duty, the keeper of  
her own conscience, answerable only to  
the law and to heaven. There will be a  
lofty observance of marriage, a noble  
race of children, only when the man  
and woman are intelligent equals and  
friends. And in that day the world  
will be ashamed to remember through  
how many centuries it ranged men into  
a mock order of devotees and women  
into a sentimental priact?

## [From _Forest and Stream; A Journal of Outdoor Life, Travel, Nature Study, Shooting, Fishing, Yachting (1873-1930)_](https://archive.org/details/sim_forest-and-stream-a-journal-of-outdoor-life_1873-09-11_1_5/page/n3/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1873-09-11 &middot; [New York](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Sn a :  
HERE the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the rugged pine, in its lonely pride.  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its mooring the old ‘canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, onc o&#x27;er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done:  
While busily back ana forth between  
  
The spider watches his silver screen,  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘* too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave.  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave  
  
And the green mass creeps o’er its dull decay:  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tome’a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower:  
While many a blossom of lovelest hue  
  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light winds play with the boat at will.  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It fioats the length of the rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time, with a careless’ hand,  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs qnick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side.  
  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,  
  
Ere the blossom waved, or the green grass grew  
  
O’ef the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/iiif/sim_forest-and-stream-a-journal-of-outdoor-life_1873-09-11_1_5&#0036;3/pct:12.905680,10.476680,20.562880,35.219479/,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

W. J. WKIGGLESWQETH, Editor and Proprietor.  
  
VOLUME XII.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And (he waters below iook dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a seabird’s wings that the storm&#x27; has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
Like the f lded hands when the work is done;  
While busily hack and forth.between,  
The spiflcr stretches hie silvery • reen,  
And thsofainni&gt;h4; with his dull ’•■too-boo,”  
Kettles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Hots slowly away in its livitig grave,  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding 1 s moldering dust away—  
Like the hand that, plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest lme  
Springs up o’er the tjteru of the old oanoe.  
The currenllcss waters are dead and still—  
lint the light wind pluys wiih the lioat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
Jt floats the length, of the-rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That mOel and part at the noon-tide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
Isy the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
i have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side—  
And looked below in the broken tide—  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
lint now, ns I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
Tin: face that i see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skill wrings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped,  
As 1 rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed.  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _Watertown republican._](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn85033295/1873-10-01/ed-1/seq-2)

#### 1873-10-01 &middot; [Watertown, Wis.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Watertown%2C_Wisconsin)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
Where the rock* are gray and the shore is steep.  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
Leans gloomily ovc r the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank.  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through.  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles arc idly dropped,  
Like&#x27;a seabird’s wings that the storm has lopped.  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
lake the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches hie silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave.  
Rots slowly away in its living grave.  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding its moldering dust away—  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of lo\e!iest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currcnticss waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noon-tide chime,  
And the shore is kisser! at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh. many a time, with a careless hand,  
1 have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick.  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side—  
And looked below in the broken tide—  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored hack from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the lisht skiif wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped,  
As I rocked w here the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grewr  
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/iiif/2/whi_carrie_ver01%2Fdata%2Fsn85033295%2F00271768746%2F1873100101%2F0122.jp2/pct:5.092528,10.328457,14.626376,21.425572/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>

through,

While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solmn owl, with his dull &quot;tco-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,

Hiding its mouldering dust away  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the grave a  
flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;

Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noon-tide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.

And paddled down where the stream runs  
quick,

thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er its rocking side  
And looked below in the broken tide  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grow,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner thinks;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped,  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass  
grew.

Where the recks are gray and the shore is steep.  
And the waters below look dark and deep.  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank.

Like the folded hands when the work is done ;  
While bupily back and forth between

Settles down on the side of the old canoe.

## [From _Forest and Stream; A Journal of Outdoor Life, Travel, Nature Study, Shooting, Fishing, Yachting (1873-1930)_](https://archive.org/details/sim_forest-and-stream-a-journal-of-outdoor-life_1873-10-16_1_10/page/n3/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1873-10-16 &middot; [New York](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
The glossy cloth that a luster gave  
  
Rots slowly away to its hidden grave;  
  
The green mildew creeps o’er its decay,  
  
Hiding the moldering past away  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles a fallen tower,  
  
While fading colors, once remote,  
  
Throw their somber hues o’er the old dress-coat.  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
  
The heaving breasts are dead and still,  
  
But the light winds play with the flaps at will;  
  
And lazily swinging its shabby tail  
  
It floats its length from a rusty nail,  
  
Like a pendulum moving the hands of time,  
  
That meet and part at the noon-tide chime,  
  
And the arms together kindly float,

## From _Ashtabula weekly telegraph._

#### 1873-10-18 &middot; [Ashtabula, Ohio](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Ashtabula%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

or sale at his yard, near the factory of  
L.M Crosby.I mulbliwa xidly 514. O. Newase,

THE OLD CANOE.  
  
Long before the war the appended simple but charm-  
ing verses appeared in a short-lived paper at Little  
Rock, Ark., without signature or address. As they  
have a tone and sentiment in keeping with the last of  
the summer months, their present revival is at least  
seasonable; and it is possible that their restoration to  
current print may elicit some tardy sign from the un­  
known bard who need never have felf ashamed to own  
himself the singer.--PiTTsDUrGH ChrONICTE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy all the day through,  
A here ties at its moorings the oid canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
elke a sea-bird&#x27;s wing that the storm has lopred,  
And crossed on the ralling, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
White busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with its dull &quot;toohoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the sides of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
lots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles a falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue,  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still-  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
floats the length of the rusted chain;  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the t hore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping how of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand.  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand.  
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,   
And lobked helow on the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below on the sluggish tide.  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I heard had a soberer tone;  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Has grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
</td></tr></table>

THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deen;  
e here the rusged pine, in its lonely pride,  
leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushe are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro&#x27;,  
There lies at its moorings the old cance.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a seabird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped;  
And croseed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silver screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
ltots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
ir the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The carrentless waters are dead and still-  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rasty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
T hat meet and part at noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddied it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whi.is are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the brokentide  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Hlave grown familiar with sterner things.  
at I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whilrs their white spray  
hed  
Ere the blossom waced, or the green grass grew  
c&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old cance

Where the rooks are gray and the shore ia sleep,  
  
And the water below look dark and deep.  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
  
t.eans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reels and rushes are long and rank.  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through  
There lies at i&#x27;s moorings tin old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped

Like the folde 1 hands when the work is .done ;  
While busy back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot; too-hoo,&quot;

Dy the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with careless hand,  
1 have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls ate wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as 1 leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tid-J,  
To fee that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old cauoo.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see is the graver grown  
And the laugh that I hear lias a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown latniliar with sterner things.  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the w ives their white spray  
shed,

■Where the rocka are gray and the shore is steep.  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride  
.Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the roods and rushes are long and rank.  
And the weds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through.  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped.  
Xdke a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped.  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one.  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery son •.i.  
And the solemn owl with hisj dull *• to &gt; ,  
Settles down on the aide of the old canoe. ,  
The stern, half-sunk, in the slimy wave,  
Rota slowly away in its living grave.  
And the green moss creeps over its dull decay.  
Hiding its moldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb of a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentloss waters are dead and still;  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out ag in  
It floats the length of tne rusty chain,  
Like the weary march ox the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew.  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Ob. many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly Strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick.  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side.  
And looked below in the broken tide.  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old oanoe.  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide.  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that! hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hand that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I love to thimc of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,
