## [From _Fayette County herald._](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn85038145/1876-01-20/ed-1/seq-1)

#### 1876-01-20 &middot; [Washington [Washington Court House], Fayette County, O. [Ohio]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Washington_Court_House%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore Is stcop&#x27;  
And the waters below&#x27;look dark and deep, :  
  
Where the rugged pine In his lofty pride  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rashes are long and rank.  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day thro,&#x27;  
  
There lies at Its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped, -  
  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lop­  
ped.And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one  
Like the foldi.d hands, when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen.  
  
And the solemn owl with his dull too-woo.&quot;  
  
Settles down on the side ef the old canoe.  
The stern, half-sank, in the slimy wave.  
  
Hots slowly away in its living grave, ;  
And the green moss creeps over Its doll decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away, &#x27; &quot; &#x27; ,  
  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er a tomb a flower.  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;  
  
While many a blossom of lovktest hue  
Springs np o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe. .  
The currentless waters are dead and still;  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will. .  
And lazily In and out again . ..  
It floats the length of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
  
That meet and part at the nonetide chime.  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew.  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time, with a careless band.  
I have pushed it way from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide.  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
Cut, now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side.  
And look below in the sluggish tide.  
The face that I sec there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone.  
And the hands that lent to the light skiffs wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things .  
But 1 love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ire the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

## The Old Canoe

## [From _The Jackson standard._](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn85038180/1876-03-16/ed-1/seq-4)

#### 1876-03-16 &middot; [Jackson C.H., Ohio](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Jackson%2C_Ohio)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
Where the rock are gray and the shore i steep.  
And the waters below look dark and deep.  
Where the rugged pine, in Us lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide; .  
Where tliarced and rushes are long and lank.  
And thlf jwtjfcfr grow thick on the winding  
haukV  
Where tbe shadow Is heavy the whole day  
through,  
There lies at Us mooring the old canoe.  
Tbe useless paddles are Idly dropped,  
Like a seu bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hask-p-ped,  
And crossed an the railing, one o&#x27;er MM.  
I.Ike the folded hands when tbe work Is done,  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches bis silvery screen,  
And the .solemn owl with the dull &quot;too-whoo,&quot;  
Hetties down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk In the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away In Its living grave.  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er his dull decay  
Hiding Its moulderliiu dust away.  
  
Like the hand that plauts o&#x27;er the tomb a  
flower,  
Or the Ivy that mantles the railing tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest line  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
Tbe currentless waters are dull and still,  
But the twilight wind plays with the tsuit at  
will.  
And lazily In and out asuln  
It floats the length of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time.  
That meet and part at the noontide rhino .  
And tbe shore Is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with reaseleas hand,  
I have pushed It away from tbe pebbly strand,  
And paddled It down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick.  
  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er rocking side,  
And looked below In the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boots were two,  
That were mirrored back from tha old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the arumbllng side,  
And look below at the sluggish tide.  
The face that I see there Is graver grown.  
And tbe laugh that I hear Is a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I lave to think of tha hours that sped  
As I rocked where tha Whirl, their white apray  
shed a  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.  
Mr and Mrs. Shott.

## The Old Cange

#### From _St. Louis globe-Democrat._

#### 1876-03-19 &middot; [St. Louis [Mo.]](http://dbpedia.org/resource/St._Louis)

[This text is not available under an open license.]

---

## [From _The Albion, A Journal of News, Politics and Literature (1822-1876)_](https://archive.org/details/sim_albion-a-journal-of-news-politics-and-literature_1876-04-01_54_14/page/n3/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1876-04-01 &middot; [New York](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And tke waters below look dark and deep,  
  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,’  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl with the dull “too whoo,”,  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe,  
  
The currentless waters are dead and atill,  
  
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time with ceaseless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick, —  
  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
‘That were mirrored back from the old canoe,  
  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed  
  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew  
  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/iiif/sim_albion-a-journal-of-news-politics-and-literature_1876-04-01_54_14&#0036;3/pct:7.001134,12.797177,20.068027,36.236999/,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

## [From _The daily free press._](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn85025540/1876-04-07/ed-1/seq-1)

#### 1876-04-07 &middot; [Trenton, N.J.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Trenton%2C_New_Jersey)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
Where the rocks are gray ami the shore is  
sleep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep:  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding  
bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has  
lopped,  
And rossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done  
While busily back and forth between  
The spicier stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with his dull “to-who.”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wav3,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps over Us dull de­

## From _Bristol news._

#### 1876-04-11 &middot; [Bristol, Va. & Tenn.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Bristol%2C_Virginia)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

intment of post-traders,

VOLUME M. BE1870L, VRGINIA A TENNESSEE, TUESDAY, APEIL 11, 4376. Whole N 553. No 33.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
We believe these beautiful verses,  
again going the rounds anonymously,  
were written by Albert Pike,-EDs.  
ALVALANCHE,  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore steep  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through;  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a seabird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with the dull &quot;too-whoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe,  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly in its living grave;  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er his dull decay  
Hiding its moulding dust away,  
Like the hands that plants o&#x27;er a tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part the noonday chime.  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew.  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe,  
Oh, many a time with ceaseless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrowed back from the old canoe.  
But now as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is grave grown,  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their whites pray shed  
Ere the blossom or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe
</td></tr></table>

## [From _Rocky Mountain husbandman._](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn83025309/1876-06-08/ed-1/seq-5)

#### 1876-06-08 &middot; [Diamond City, Mont.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Great_Falls%2C_Montana)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

THE HO0E CIRCLE.  
THE OLD CANOE.  
ALBERT PIKE.  
Where the rocks are gray, and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a seabird&#x27;s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with the dull &quot; too-whoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave;  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er his dull decay  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er a tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of lovliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noonday chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with ceaseless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/iiif/2/mthi_jay_ver01%2Fdata%2Fsn83025309%2F00294551864%2F1876060801%2F0260.jp2/pct:4.211957,4.963680,21.625906,43.205206/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>

## [From _The Woman's Journal 1870-1916_](https://archive.org/details/sim_the-womans-journal_1879-04-05_10_14/page/n1/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1879-04-05 &middot; [Boston, Massachusetts](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Boston)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide:  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a seabird’s wings that the storm hath lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between,  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull ‘‘too hoo,”  
Nestles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots elowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the moldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plauts o’er the tomb a flower,  
Of the ivy that mantles a fallen tower;  
  
While many a blossom of liveliest hue  
  
Springs up o’er the bow of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
  
But the light winds play with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the bands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream ran quick—  
Where the whirls were wild and the storm was thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe,  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear has a sober tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things.  
But I like to think of the hours that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls their wild spray threw  
Ere the blossoms moved or the green grass grew  
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/iiif/sim_the-womans-journal_1879-04-05_10_14&#0036;1/pct:9.936407,9.590261,15.937997,27.998812/,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

## [From _Forest and Stream; A Journal of Outdoor Life, Travel, Nature Study, Shooting, Fishing, Yachting (1873-1930)_](https://archive.org/details/sim_forest-and-stream-a-journal-of-outdoor-life_1879-05-15_12_15/page/n0/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1879-05-15 &middot; [New York](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
pure and unbroken as a mirror, with its wooded shores gleam-  
ing aloft in unnumbered hues and unrivalled radiance, which  
autumn nowhere sbeds with so lavish a hand as in an Amer-  
ican woods, and sleeping below, reflected to the smallest leaf  
on the calm surface of the water. Attached to the bridge by  
a long chain was an old waterlogged canoe, which had once  
been used as a fishing boat, but now it was almost ready to  
fall to pieces from old age :—  
“ The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave ;  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
  
Hiding the mouldering dust away. .  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
  
Or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower ;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
“ The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will ;  
And lazily, in and out again,  
  
It floats tne length of the ruaty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime.  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.”  
  
The air was breathless, yet so pure and fresh that the brisk-  
est breeze that ever fanned the ocean came not more grate-  
fully to the cheek and brow than that delicious calm in which  
the most delicate leaf alone might be seen to quiver, while al

## From _Memphis daily appeal._

#### 1883-05-13 &middot; [Memphis, Tenn.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Memphis%2C_Tennessee)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

of the cyclone.  
One of the most pleasant features of the con  
cert was the beautiful singing of the little  
seven-year-old twin daughters of the Hon. H.  
Q. Bridges, of Summit. These sweet little  
girls are as much alike as two &quot;black-eyed  
peas, and are indeed prodigies. We hear  
that they will be put on the stage soon, and  
then no doubt Memphis will have an oppor  
tunity to listen to their wonderful singing.  
The Federal Court is grinding along  
rapidly. The Supreme Court has had up the  
docket of the Northern District this week,  
and there are many prominent lawyers in the  
city.WILSOnA.

Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay  
Hiding the moldering dust away  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a flower,  
or the ivy that manties the inliins towel,  
While many blossoms of the loveliest hue  
spring up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dull and still,  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And laztly in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain;  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
By the dipping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddied it down where the stream run  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see is the graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a silvery tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped,  
As I rocked where the waves their white spray  
shed,  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe
</td></tr></table>

---

## From _The Palatka daily news._

#### 1885-05-31 &middot; [Palatka, Fla.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Palatka%2C_Florida)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is  
  
steep,  
  
And the waters below look dark and deep:  
  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
  
through,  
  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the wind has lop  
  
ped,  
  
And crossed on the railing one o&#x27;er one,  
  
Like folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back aud forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull too-whoo,  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss cfeeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust away.  
  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a  
  
flower,  
  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
  
While many a blossom of the loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
  
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at  
  
will:  
  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
  
By the dripping bows of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time with careless hand,  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
  
quick,  
  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
  
thick,  
  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
  
And looked below in the broken tide  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
that were mirrowed back from the old canoe.  
  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff  
  
wings  
  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
  
But I love to think of the hours that,  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white  
  
spray shed,  
  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass  
  
grew  
  
O&#x27;e the mouldering stern of the old canoe,

## From _Middlebury register._

#### 1886-05-07 &middot; [Middlebury, Vt.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Middlebury%2C_Vermont)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

he elm; for experience ha  
proved that, to insure success in the con  
tinued cultivation of trees or herbaceon  
plants on the same soil, the practice mu  
be varied with species of different genera  
Nuts of the European walnut and of  
the black walnut have been planted  
the same time in the same soil; those of  
the black walnut are observed to shoot  
more vigorously and grow in a given  
time to a greater height. By graftin  
the European upon the American specie  
at the height of eight or ten feet their ad  
vantages with respect to the quality both  
of wood and of fruit might be united.  
pOEIRY.  
ThE OLD CANOR.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore i  
steel,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the ragged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are tall and  
rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding  
bank,  
Where the shadows are heavy the whole day  
thotigH,  
Lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm hath lop  
ped.  
nd crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silver sereen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull &quot;too-hoo,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
ots slowly away in its living grave;  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb  
flower,  
or the ivy that mantles the fallen tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
prings up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light winds play with the boat at will  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
O, many a time, with a careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddys are  
thick.  
And longed as I leaned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were fico  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone  
And the hands that lent to the light skinf  
wings  
Have grown familiar with stemer things,  
But I love to think of the houis that flew,  
As I rocked where the whirls teir white spray  
threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green moss  
erew.  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe  
EMIlY R. Pag Braord, YT.

## [From _Current Literature (1888-1912)_](https://archive.org/details/sim_current-opinion_1889-03_2_3/page/n49/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1889-03-01 &middot; [New York](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
  
The Old Canoe—Emily Rebecca Page  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide ;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
Lies at the moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between,  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen ;  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “ too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the sides of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half-sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding the mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower ;  
  
While many a blossom of the loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still—  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again,  
  
It floats the length of its rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noon-tide chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew  
  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Many a time, with careless hand  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick—  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick—  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things ;  
  
But I love to think of the hours that flew  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray threw,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/iiif/sim_current-opinion_1889-03_2_3&#0036;49/pct:21.074879,12.335382,30.887681,60.250219/,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

## [From _The Louisiana Democrat._](https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/lccn/sn82003389/1889-08-14/ed-1/seq-4)

#### 1889-08-14 &middot; [Alexandria, La.](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Alexandria%2C_Louisiana)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

?he oauisiala fmocrat.  
PUBLISHED EVERY WEDNESDAY.  
ALEXANDRIA. LOUISIANA.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is  
steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine, In its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding  
bank;  
Where the shadow Is heavy the whole day  
through.  
There lies at its mooring the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
Like a sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has  
lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o&#x27;er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl with the dull &quot;too-whoe,&quot;  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk in the glimy wave,  
Rots slowly away on its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o&#x27;er its null decay,  
Hiding its mouldering days away.  
Like the hand that plants o&#x27;er the tomb a  
flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
The twilivht wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the length of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part with the mountain chime,  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the drippling bough of the old canoe.  
Oh! many a time with ceaseless hand  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand.  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
And laughed as I learned o&#x27;er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the laces and boats were two,  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff  
wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white  
spray shed  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass  
grew  
O&#x27;er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://chroniclingamerica.loc.gov/iiif/2/lu_beast_ver01%2Fdata%2Fsn82003389%2F00294555195%2F1889081401%2F0141.jp2/pct:3.902176,4.281902,13.342924,31.210577/!600,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white  
spray shed  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass  
grew  
O&#x27;er the moldering stern of the old canoe.

## [From _Good Housekeeping 1885-2015_](https://archive.org/details/sim_good-housekeeping_good-housekeeping_1889-09-14_9_10/page/n23/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1889-09-14 &middot; [Springfield, Massachusetts](http://dbpedia.org/resource/Springfield%2C_Massachusetts)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

dow and shine,  
A glory of olive and amber wine,  
To the westering sun the colors run  
Through the deeps of the ripening wheat.  
  
O glorious land! My Western land,  
Outspread beneath the setting sun!  
Once more amid your swells I stand,  
And cross your sod lands dry and dun,  
I hear the jocund calls of men  
Who sweep amid the ripened grain  
With swift, stern reapers, once again.  
  
The evening splendor floods the plain,  
The cricket’s chime  
Makes pauseless rhyme,  
And toward the sun  
The splendid colors romp and run  
Before the wind’s feet  
In the wheat !  
  
—Hamlin Garland, in Youth&#x27;s Companion.  
  
THE OLD CANOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank ;  
  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,  
There lies at its mooring the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea bird’s wings that the storm has  
lopped,  
  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
  
While busily, back and forth between,  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl with the dull ‘‘ too-whoo,”  
  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding its moldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower,  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
  
But the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh! many a time with careless hand,  
  
I have pushed it away from the pelly strand,  
  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are  
thick,  
  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
  
shed,  
Ere the blossom waved or the green grass grew  
O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/iiif/sim_good-housekeeping_good-housekeeping_1889-09-14_9_10&#0036;23/pct:44.406675,10.422470,49.876391,80.037140/,600/0/default.jpg"/>

| WHERE the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
  
| And the waters below look dark and deep;  
  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,

The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between

And the solemn owl with the dull “ too-whoo,”  
Sottles down on the side of the old canoe,  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding its moldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe,

Lut the twilight wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe,

Oh! many a time with ceaseless hand  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o’er its rocking side,  
  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe,

THE OLD C.iXOE.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is  
steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
Where the tugged pine, iu its lonely pride,  
Lewis gloomily over the murky tide;  
Where the reeds and rushes are loug and  
liuik,  
And the weeds grow thick on the wiuding  
bunk;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,

The useless paddles nrjj idly dropped,  
Like a sea-bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has  
lopped.  
And crossed on the railiug, one o&#x27;er one,

&#x27;&quot; done,

The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with the dull &quot;too­  
whoo,&quot;Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
The stern half sunk iu the slimy wave,

And the green moss creeps -o&#x27;er its dull  
decay,  
Hiding its molderiug dust away,  
Like the hand that plant o&#x27;er the tomb a  
flower.  
Or the ivy that nmutles tlm falling tower.  
While many a oblssoia of loveliest hue  
Springs up o&#x27;er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentlcss waters are dead and still,  
Jut the twilight wind plays with the boat at  
will,  
And lazily in and out.agaiu  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime,  
And the shore is kied tit each turning  
anew  
By the dripping bough of the old canoe.  
Oh! many a time with careless hand  
I have pushed it uway from the pebbly  
strand&#x27;  
And paddled it dowu where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wild and the eddies  
are thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o&#x27;er rocking bide,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old  
cauoe.  
Rut now, as I lean o&#x27;er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone,

wings

## [From _Current Literature (1888-1912)_](https://archive.org/details/sim_current-opinion_1897-05_21_5/page/n32/mode/1up?view=theater)

#### 1897-05-01 &middot; [New York](http://dbpedia.org/resource/New_York_City)

<table style="width: 100%;"><tr><td style="width: 50%">

  
  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep;  
  
Where the rugged pine in its lonely pride,  
  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank;  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
  
The useless paddles are idly dropped,  
  
Like a sea-bird’s wings that the storm has lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
  
Like the folded hands when the work is done;  
While busily back and forth between  
  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
The stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
  
And the green moss creeps o’er the dull decay  
Hiding its mouldering dust away,  
  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
  
It floats the length of the rusty chain,  
  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turn anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
  
Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs quick,  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies thick,  
\nd laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side,  
And looked below in the broken tide  
  
To see that the faces and boat were two  
  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
  
But now as I lean o’er the crumbling side  
  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
  
And the hands that lent to the light skiff wings  
Have grown familiar with sterner things,  
  
But I love to think of the hours sped  
  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray shed,  
Ere the blossoms waved or the green grass grew  
O’er the mouldering stern of the old canoe.
</td><td style="width: 50%; max-height: 75%; margin: auto; display: block;">
<img alt="Page image" src="https://iiif.archive.org/iiif/sim_current-opinion_1897-05_21_5&#0036;32/pct:51.222494,11.578947,31.754279,60.504386/,600/0/default.jpg"/>
</td>
</tr></table>

home comsu motion.  
These mills furnish work for many

Mena.  
Our Town Assessment All Right.  
The city attorney was notified last  
Saturday by County Clerk Davis that  
the town tax assessment hail been put  
upon the tax book and was all O, K.  
This action was taken as a result of  
an opinion handed down by a couple  
of eminent jurists of Little Rock.  
  
The Old Canoe.  
Where the rocks are gray and the shore is  
steep.  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide;  
Imre the reeds and the rushes are long and  
lank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding  
bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
through,  
There lies at is mooring the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are Idly dropped,  
Like the sea bird&#x27;s wings that the storm has  
lopped,  
And crossed on the railing, one o’er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work i» done,  
While busily back and forth between.  
The spider stretches his silvery screen  
And the solemn owl with the dull &quot;too  
whoo,”  
Settles down on the edge of the old eanoe.  
The stem half sunk iri the slimy wave,  
Hots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss ereap o’er Its dull decay,  
Hiding its mouldering dust swev.  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a  
flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower,  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue.  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
Hut the twilight wind plays with the boat at  
will.  
And lazily in and out again,  
It floats the length of the rusty chain ;  
Like the weary march of the hand of time.  
That meet and part at the noon tide chime,  
  
And the shore is kissed nt each turning  
anew,  
By the dripping bow of the old canoe.  
Oh, many a time with careless hand,  
I have pushed it away from the pebbly strand.  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick.  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies are  
thick.  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocking side  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that faces and imats were two  
That were mirrored back from the old canoe.  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below hi the sluggish tide.  
The face that I see there is graver grown.  
And the laugh that I hear is a sober tone.  
Tlie bands that lent to the light skiff wings.  
Have grown familiar with sterner things:  
But 1 love to think of the hours that sped,  
As 1 rocked where the whirls their white spray  
shed.

grew,

Where the rocks are gray and the shore is steep,  
And the waters below look dark and deep,  
Where the rugged pine, in its lonely pride,  
Leans gloomily over the murky tide,  
Where the reeds and rushes are long and rank,  
And the weeds grow thick on the winding bank,  
Where the shadow is heavy the whole day  
  
through,  
There lies at its moorings the old canoe.  
The useless paddles are idly dropped.  
Like seabird’s wings that the storms had lopped,  
And crossed on the railing one o’er one,  
Like the folded hands when the work is done,  
While busily back and forth between  
The spider stretches his silvery screen,  
And the solemn owl, with his dull “too-hoo,”  
Settles down on the side of the old canoe.  
  
Th« stern, half sunk in the slimy wave,  
Rots slowly away in its living grave,  
And the green moss creeps o’er its dull decay,  
Hiding its mol’dring dust away,  
Like the hand that plants o’er the tomb a flower,  
Or the ivy that mantles the falling tower;  
While many a blossom of loveliest hue  
Springs up o’er the stern of the old canoe.  
  
The currentless waters are dead and still,  
But the light wind plays with the boat at will,  
And lazily in and out again  
It floats the lengths of the rusty chain.  
Like the weary march of the hands of time,  
That meet and part at the noontide chime;  
And the shore is kissed at each turning anew,  
*By the rippling bow of the old canoe.  
  
•Oh, many a time, with a careless hand,  
T have pushed it away from the pebbly strand,  
And paddled it down where the stream runs  
quick,  
Where the whirls are wide and the eddies are  
  
thick,  
And laughed as I leaned o’er the rocky side,  
And looked below in the broken tide,  
To see that the faces and boats were two,  
That where mirrored back from the old canoe,  
  
But now, as I lean o’er the crumbling side,  
And look below in the sluggish tide,  
The face that I see there is graver grown,  
And the laugh that I hear has a soberer tone,  
And the hands that lend to the light skiff wings,  
Have grown familiar with sterner things;  
But I love to think of the hours that sped  
As I rocked where the whirls their white spray  
  
shed,  
Ere the blossoms waved, or the green grass grew  
•O’er the moldering stern of the old canoe
